1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/40px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Sino-Roman relations</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Romano-Chinese_relations&amp;redirect=no" title="Romano-Chinese relations">Romano-Chinese relations</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:404px;max-width:404px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomanandHanEmpiresAD1.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="2214" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/RomanandHanEmpiresAD1.png/400px-RomanandHanEmpiresAD1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/RomanandHanEmpiresAD1.png/600px-RomanandHanEmpiresAD1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/RomanandHanEmpiresAD1.png/800px-RomanandHanEmpiresAD1.png 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han Empire</a> around <a href="/wiki/AD_1" title="AD 1">AD 1</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_050ad.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1903" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/East-Hem_050ad.jpg/400px-East-Hem_050ad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/East-Hem_050ad.jpg/600px-East-Hem_050ad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/East-Hem_050ad.jpg/800px-East-Hem_050ad.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">Eastern Hemisphere</a> in <a href="/wiki/AD_50" title="AD 50">AD 50</a>, the middle of the first century</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Periplous_of_the_Erythraean_Sea.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="903" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Periplous_of_the_Erythraean_Sea.svg/400px-Periplous_of_the_Erythraean_Sea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Periplous_of_the_Erythraean_Sea.svg/600px-Periplous_of_the_Erythraean_Sea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Periplous_of_the_Erythraean_Sea.svg/800px-Periplous_of_the_Erythraean_Sea.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The trade relations between Rome and the East, including China, according to the 1st-century AD navigation guide <i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i></div></div></div></div>\n<p><b>Sino-Roman relations</b> comprised the mostly indirect contact, flow of trade goods, information, and occasional travellers between the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han Empire</a> of China, as well as between the later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Dynasties_in_Chinese_history" title="Dynasties in Chinese history">Chinese dynasties</a>. These empires inched progressively closer in the course of the Roman expansion into the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near East</a> and simultaneous Han Chinese military incursions into <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>. Mutual awareness remained low, and firm knowledge about each other was limited. Only a few attempts at direct contact are known from records. Intermediate empires such as the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushans</a>, seeking to maintain lucrative control over the silk trade, inhibited direct contact between these two <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasian</a> powers. In 97 AD, the <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_China_before_1911" title="Military history of China before 1911">Chinese</a> general <a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a> tried to send his envoy <a href="/wiki/Gan_Ying" title="Gan Ying">Gan Ying</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a>, but Gan was dissuaded by <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a> from venturing beyond the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>. Several alleged Roman emissaries to China were recorded by ancient Chinese historians. The first one on record, supposedly from either the Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> or his adopted son <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>, arrived in 166 AD. Others are recorded as arriving in 226 and 284 AD, with a long absence until the first recorded <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_diplomacy" title="Byzantine diplomacy">Byzantine embassy</a> in 643 AD.\n</p><p>The indirect exchange of goods on land along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo%E2%80%93Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo&#8211;Roman trade relations">sea routes</a> included Chinese <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glassware</a> and high-quality cloth. <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman coins</a> minted from the 1st century AD onwards have been found in China, as well as a coin of <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medal" title="Medal">medallions</a> from the reigns of Antoninus Pius and Marcus Aurelius in <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> in modern Vietnam, the same region at which Chinese sources claim the Romans first landed. Roman glassware and silverware have been discovered at Chinese archaeological sites dated to the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">Han period</a>. Roman coins and glass beads have also been found in Japan.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical</a> sources, the problem of identifying references to ancient China is exacerbated by the interpretation of the Latin term <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seres" title="Seres">Seres</a></i>, whose meaning fluctuated and could refer to several Asian peoples in a wide arc from India over Central Asia to China. In Chinese records, the Roman Empire came to be known as <i><a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a></i> or Great Qin. Daqin was directly associated with the later <i>Fulin</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#25282;&#33787;</span>) in Chinese sources, which has been identified by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hirth" title="Friedrich Hirth">Friedrich Hirth</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Chinese sources describe several embassies of Fulin arriving in China during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> and also mention the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674-678)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674-678)">siege of Constantinople</a> by the forces of <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah I</a> in 674&#8211;678 AD.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_geographers" title="Roman geographers">Geographers in the Roman Empire</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> provided a rough sketch of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a> and beyond this the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Thailand" title="Gulf of Thailand">Gulf of Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>. Ptolemy\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattigara" title="Cattigara">Cattigara</a> was most likely <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="&#211;c Eo">&#211;c Eo</a>, Vietnam, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonine" title="Antonine">Antonine</a>-era Roman items have been found. Ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_geography" title="Chinese geography">Chinese geographers</a> demonstrated a general knowledge of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Rome\'s eastern provinces</a>. The 7th-century AD Byzantine historian <a href="/wiki/Theophylact_Simocatta" title="Theophylact Simocatta">Theophylact Simocatta</a> wrote of the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">reunification</a> of <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_southern_China" title="Northern and southern China">northern and southern China</a>, which he treated as <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">separate nations recently at war</a>. This mirrors both the <a href="/wiki/Chen_dynasty" title="Chen dynasty">conquest of Chen</a> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wen_of_Sui" title="Emperor Wen of Sui">Emperor Wen of Sui</a> (reigned 581&#8211;604 AD) as well as the names <i><a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manzi_(geography)" title="Manzi (geography)">Mangi</a></i> used by later <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">medieval Europeans in China</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han-Chinese</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song_dynasty" title="Southern Song dynasty">Southern Song dynasty</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_accounts_and_cartography">Geographical accounts and cartography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geographical accounts and cartography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_geography">Roman geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Roman geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Graeco-Roman_geographers" title="List of Graeco-Roman geographers">List of Graeco-Roman geographers</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_cartography" title="History of cartography">History of cartography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serica" title="Serica">Serica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">Indo-Roman trade relations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PtolemyWorldMap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1026" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/220px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/330px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/440px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PtolemyWorldMap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> reconstruction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_Geography" title="Ptolemy\'s Geography">Ptolemy</a>\'s 1st projection, indicating the Land of Silk (<i><a href="/wiki/Serica" title="Serica">Serica</a></i>) in northeast Asia at the end of the overland Silk Road and the land of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin_Empire" title="Qin Empire">Qin</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinae" title="Sinae">Sinae</a></i>) in the southeast at the end of the maritime routes; 1450&#8211;1475 AD, attributed to <a href="/wiki/Francesco_di_Antonio_del_Chierico" title="Francesco di Antonio del Chierico">Francesco del Chierico</a> and translated from Greek to Latin by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Emanuel_Chrysoloras&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Emanuel Chrysoloras (page does not exist)">Emanuel Chrysoloras</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacobus_Angelus" title="Jacobus Angelus">Jacobus Angelus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="1787" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg/220px-Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg/330px-Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg/440px-Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_Asia_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> reconstruction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>\'s 11th Asian regional map with the Gulf of the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> to the left, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Peninsula" title="Golden Peninsula">Golden Peninsula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malaysia</a>) in the centre, and the <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Sinus" title="Magnus Sinus">Great Gulf</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Thailand" title="Gulf of Thailand">Gulf of Thailand</a>) to the right; the land of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinae" title="Sinae">Sinae</a> is positioned on its northern and eastern shores.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="2214" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png/220px-Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png/330px-Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png/440px-Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_HanEmpiresAD1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> in <a href="/wiki/AD_1" title="AD 1">1 AD</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> (red), <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> (brown), Chinese <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han Empire</a> (yellow), and <a href="/wiki/Middle_kingdoms_of_India" title="Middle kingdoms of India">Indian kingdoms</a>, smaller states (light yellow)</div></div></div>\n<p>Beginning in the 1st century BC with <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, Roman histories offer only vague accounts of China and the silk-producing Seres people of the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>, who were perhaps the ancient Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ostrovsky_2007_p44_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ostrovsky_2007_p44-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_2007_143_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis_2007_143-3">[3]</a></sup> The 1st-century AD geographer <a href="/wiki/Pomponius_Mela" title="Pomponius Mela">Pomponius Mela</a> asserted that the lands of the Seres formed the centre of the coast of <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">an eastern ocean</a>, flanked to the south by India and to the north by the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">Eurasian Steppe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ostrovsky_2007_p44_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ostrovsky_2007_p44-2">[2]</a></sup> The 2nd-century AD Roman historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florus" title="Florus">Florus</a> seems to have confused the Seres with peoples of India, or at least noted that their skin complexions proved that they both lived "beneath another sky" than the Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ostrovsky_2007_p44_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-ostrovsky_2007_p44-2">[2]</a></sup> Roman authors generally seem to have been confused about where the Seres were located, in either Central Asia or East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a> (c. 330 &#8211; c. 400 AD) wrote that the land of the Seres was enclosed by great natural walls around a river called Bautis, possibly a description of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ostrovsky_2007_p44_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-ostrovsky_2007_p44-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The existence of China was known to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_cartographers" title="Roman cartographers">Roman cartographers</a>, but their understanding of it was less certain. Ptolemy\'s 2nd-century AD <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_Geography" title="Ptolemy\'s Geography">Geography</a></i> separates the Land of Silk (<i>Serica</i>) at the end of the overland <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> from the land of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin_Empire" title="Qin Empire">Qin</a> (<i>Sinae</i>) reached by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Sinae are placed on the northern shore of the <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Sinus" title="Magnus Sinus">Great Gulf</a> (<i>Magnus Sinus</i>) east of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Peninsula" title="Golden Peninsula">Golden Peninsula</a> (<i>Aurea Chersonesus</i>, Malay Peninsula). Their chief port, Cattigara, seems to have been in the lower <a href="/wiki/Mekong_Delta" title="Mekong Delta">Mekong Delta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup> The Great Gulf served as a combined <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Thailand" title="Gulf of Thailand">Gulf of Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Marinus_of_Tyre" title="Marinus of Tyre">Marinus of Tyre</a> and Ptolemy\'s belief that the Indian Ocean was an inland sea caused them to bend the <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodian</a> coast south beyond the equator before turning west to join southern <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libya</a> (<a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2010_pp58-59_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2010_pp58-59-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Much of this is given as <a href="/wiki/Terra_incognita" title="Terra incognita">unknown lands</a>, but the north-eastern area is placed under the Sinae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Classical geographers such as <a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Strabo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> were slow to incorporate new information into their works and, from their positions as <a href="/wiki/Auctoritas" title="Auctoritas">esteemed scholars</a>, were seemingly prejudiced against lowly merchants and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topographical" title="Topographical">topographical</a> accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker_2008_p118_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-parker_2008_p118-10">[10]</a></sup> Ptolemy\'s work represents a break from this, since he demonstrated an openness to their accounts and would not have been able to chart the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> so accurately without the input of traders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker_2008_p118_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-parker_2008_p118-10">[10]</a></sup> In the 1st-century AD <i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i>, its anonymous Greek-speaking author, a merchant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>, provides such vivid accounts of eastern trade cities that it is clear he visited many of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoff_1912_intro_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-schoff_1912_intro-11">[11]</a></sup> These include sites in Arabia, Pakistan, and India, including <a href="/wiki/Guide_book" title="Guide book">travel times from rivers and towns</a>, where to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchorage_(shipping)" title="Anchorage (shipping)">drop anchor</a>, the locations of royal courts, lifestyles of the locals and goods found in their markets, and favourable times of year to sail from Egypt to these places to catch the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoff_1912_intro_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-schoff_1912_intro-11">[11]</a></sup> The <i>Periplus</i> also mentions a great inland city, <i>Thinae</i> (or <i>Sinae</i>), in a country called <i>This</i> that perhaps stretched as far as the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoff_2004_paragraph64_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-schoff_2004_paragraph64-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_footnote2_p43_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_footnote2_p43-13">[13]</a></sup> The text notes that silk produced there travelled to neighbouring India via the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> by a land route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoff_2004_paragraph64_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-schoff_2004_paragraph64-12">[12]</a></sup> Marinus and Ptolemy had relied on the testimony of a Greek sailor named Alexander, probably a merchant, for how to reach Cattigara (most likely &#211;c Eo, Vietnam).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawer_2013_p38_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-mawer_2013_p38-14">[14]</a></sup> Alexander (Greek: Alexandros) mentions that the main terminus for Roman traders was a Burmese city called Tamala on the north-west Malay Peninsula, where Indian merchants travelled overland across the <a href="/wiki/Kra_Isthmus" title="Kra Isthmus">Kra Isthmus</a> to reach the Perimulic Gulf (the Gulf of <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2014_p205_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2014_p205-15">[15]</a></sup> Alexandros claimed that it took twenty days to sail from Thailand to a port called "Zabia" (or <i>Zaba</i>) in southern Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2014_p205_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2014_p205-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suarez_1999_p90_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-suarez_1999_p90-16">[16]</a></sup> According to him, one could continue along the coast (of southern Vietnam) from Zabia until reaching the trade port of Cattigara after an unspecified number of days (with "some" being interpreted as "many" by Marinus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2014_p205_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2014_p205-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suarez_1999_p90_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-suarez_1999_p90-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cosmas_Indicopleustes" title="Cosmas Indicopleustes">Cosmas Indicopleustes</a>, a 6th-century AD Eastern Roman (Byzantine) Greek monk from <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and former merchant with experience in the Indian Ocean trade, was the first Roman to write clearly about China in his <i><a href="/wiki/Christian_Topography" title="Christian Topography">Christian Topography</a></i> (c. 550 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He called it the country of <i><a href="/wiki/Names_of_China" title="Names of China">Tzinista</a></i> (comparable to <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>Chinasthana</i> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> <i>Sinistan</i> from the 781 AD <a href="/wiki/Nestorian_Stele" title="Nestorian Stele">Nestorian Stele</a> of <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a>, China), located in easternmost Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p28_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p28-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> He explained the maritime route towards it (first sailing east and then north up the southern coast of the Asian continent) and the fact that <a href="/wiki/Clove" title="Clove">cloves</a> came that way to <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> for sale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p28_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p28-18">[18]</a></sup> By the time of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperor" title="Byzantine emperor">the Eastern Roman</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> (r. 527&#8211;565 AD), the Byzantines purchased Chinese silk from <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> intermediaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luttwak_2008_p168_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-luttwak_2008_p168-20">[20]</a></sup> They also <a href="/wiki/Smuggling_of_silkworm_eggs_into_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Smuggling of silkworm eggs into the Byzantine Empire">smuggled silkworms out of China</a> with the help of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian" title="Nestorian">Nestorian</a> monks, who claimed that the land of <a href="/wiki/Serindia" title="Serindia">Serindia</a> was located north of India and produced the finest silk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luttwak_2008_p168_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-luttwak_2008_p168-20">[20]</a></sup> By smuggling silkworms and producing silk of their own, the Byzantines could bypass the Chinese silk trade dominated by their chief rivals, the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luttwak_2008_pp168-169_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-luttwak_2008_pp168-169-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From <a href="/wiki/Turkic_Khaganate" title="Turkic Khaganate">Turkic peoples</a> of Central Asia during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a> (386&#8211;535 AD) period the Eastern Romans acquired yet another <a href="/wiki/Names_of_China" title="Names of China">name for China</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Tuoba" title="Tuoba">Taugast</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_language" title="Old Turkic language">Old Turkic</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Tuoba" title="Tuoba">Tabghach</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luttwak_2008_p168_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-luttwak_2008_p168-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theophylact_Simocatta" title="Theophylact Simocatta">Theophylact Simocatta</a>, a historian during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> (r. 610&#8211;641 AD), wrote that Taugast (or Taugas) was a great eastern empire <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">colonised by Turkic people</a>, with a capital city 2,400 kilometres (1,500&#160;mi) northeast of India that he called <i>Khubdan</i> (from the Turkic word <i>Khumdan</i> used for the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a> and Tang capital <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a>), where <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">idolatry was practised</a> but the people were wise and lived by just laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> He depicted the Chinese empire as being divided by a great river (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangzi" title="Yangzi">Yangzi</a>) that served as the boundary between <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">two rival nations at war</a>; during the reign of Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Maurice_(emperor)" title="Maurice (emperor)">Maurice</a> (582&#8211;602 AD) the northerners wearing "black coats" conquered the "red coats" of the south (black being a distinctive colour worn by the people of <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, location of the Sui capital Sui Chang\'an, according to the 16th-century Persian traveller Hajji Mahomed, or Chaggi Memet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p30_and_footnote2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p30_and_footnote2-23">[23]</a></sup> This account may correspond to the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Chen_dynasty" title="Chen dynasty">Chen dynasty</a> and reunification of China by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wen_of_Sui" title="Emperor Wen of Sui">Emperor Wen of Sui</a> (r. 581&#8211;604 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p30_and_footnote2_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p30_and_footnote2-23">[23]</a></sup> Simocatta names their ruler as <i>Taisson</i>, which he claimed meant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_emperor" title="Chinese emperor">Son of God</a>, either correlating to the Chinese <i>Tianzi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Son_of_Heaven" title="Son of Heaven">Son of Heaven</a>) or even the name of the contemporary ruler <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a> (r. 626 &#8211; 649 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Later <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">medieval Europeans in China</a> wrote of it as two separate countries, with <i><a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a></i> in the north and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manzi_(geography)" title="Manzi (geography)">Mangi</a></i> in the south, during the period when the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> led by Mongol ruler <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> (r. 1260&#8211;1294 AD) conquered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song_Dynasty" title="Southern Song Dynasty">Southern Song Dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_geography">Chinese geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chinese geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_Chinese_cartography" title="Early Chinese cartography">Early Chinese cartography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_geography" title="Chinese geography">Chinese geography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pei_Xiu" title="Pei Xiu">Pei Xiu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Daoyi_Zhil%C3%BCe" title="Daoyi Zhil&#252;e">Daoyi Zhil&#252;e</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3549" data-file-width="3675" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG/220px-Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG/330px-Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG/440px-Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> silk map found in tomb 3 of <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui Han tombs site</a>, depicting the <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Kings of the Han dynasty">kingdom</a> of <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a> and Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Nanyue" title="Nanyue">Nanyue</a> (Vietnam) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_China" title="Southern China">southern China</a> (with the south oriented at the top), 2nd century BC</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1746" data-file-width="2331" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg/220px-SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg/330px-SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg/440px-SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SihaiHuayiZongtu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqinguo</a> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#22823;&#31206;&#22283;</span>) appears at the Western edge of this <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> Chinese <a href="/wiki/World_map" title="World map">world map</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sihai_Huayi_Zongtu" title="Sihai Huayi Zongtu">Sihai Huayi Zongtu</a></i>, published in 1532 AD.</div></div></div>\n<p>Detailed geographical information about the Roman Empire, at least its easternmost territories, is provided in traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">Chinese historiography</a>. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Shiji</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> (c. 145&#8211;86 BC) gives descriptions of countries in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a>. These accounts became significantly more nuanced in the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Book of Han</a></i>, co-authored by <a href="/wiki/Ban_Gu" title="Ban Gu">Ban Gu</a> and his sister <a href="/wiki/Ban_Zhao" title="Ban Zhao">Ban Zhao</a>, younger siblings of the general <a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a>, who led military exploits <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_the_Western_Regions" title="Protectorate of the Western Regions">into Central Asia</a> before returning to China in 102 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> The westernmost territories of Asia as described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Later_Han" title="Book of the Later Han">Book of the Later Han</a></i> compiled by <a href="/wiki/Fan_Ye_(historian)" title="Fan Ye (historian)">Fan Ye</a> (398&#8211;445 AD) formed the basis for almost all later accounts of Daqin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[note 1]</a></sup> These accounts seem to be restricted to descriptions of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/History_of_Syria" title="History of Syria">Syria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> Historical linguist <a href="/wiki/Edwin_G._Pulleyblank" title="Edwin G. Pulleyblank">Edwin G. Pulleyblank</a> explains that Chinese historians considered Daqin to be a kind of "counter-China" located at the opposite end of their known world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pulleyblank_1999_p71_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pulleyblank_1999_p71-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-see_also_lewis_2007_143_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-see_also_lewis_2007_143-31">[30]</a></sup> According to Pulleyblank, "the Chinese conception of D&#224; Q&#237;n was confused from the outset with ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">mythological notions</a> about the far west".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pulleyblank_1999,_p._78_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pulleyblank_1999,_p._78-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-see_also_lewis_2007_143_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-see_also_lewis_2007_143-31">[30]</a></sup> The Chinese histories explicitly related Daqin and Lijian (also "Li-kan", or Syria) as belonging to the same country; according to Yule, D. D. Leslie, and K. H. G. Gardiner, the earliest descriptions of Lijian in the <i>Shiji</i> distinguished it as the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic-era</a> <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-john_hill_houhanshu_2003_web_notes_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-john_hill_houhanshu_2003_web_notes-35">[34]</a></sup> Pulleyblank provides some linguistic analysis to dispute their proposal, arguing that Tiaozhi (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#26465;&#25903;</span>) in the <i>Shiji</i> was most likely the Seleucid Empire and that Lijian, although still poorly understood, could be identified with either <a href="/wiki/Hyrcania" title="Hyrcania">Hyrcania</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Iran</a> or even <a href="/wiki/History_of_Alexandria" title="History of Alexandria">Alexandria in Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Weil%C3%BCe" title="Weil&#252;e">Weil&#252;e</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Yu_Huan" title="Yu Huan">Yu Huan</a> (c. 239&#8211;265 AD), preserved in <a href="/wiki/Annotations_to_Records_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Annotations to Records of the Three Kingdoms">annotations to</a> the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Records of the Three Kingdoms">Records of the Three Kingdoms</a></i> (published in 429 AD by <a href="/wiki/Pei_Songzhi" title="Pei Songzhi">Pei Songzhi</a>), also provides details about the easternmost portion of the Roman world, including mention of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> For Roman Egypt, the book explains the location of Alexandria, travelling distances along the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> and the tripartite division of the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heptanomis" title="Heptanomis">Heptanomis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thebaid" title="Thebaid">Thebaid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Zhu_Fan_Zhi" title="Zhu Fan Zhi">Zhu Fan Zhi</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Quanzhou</a> customs inspector <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhao_Rugua" title="Zhao Rugua">Zhao Rugua</a> (1170&#8211;1228 AD) described the ancient <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse_of_Alexandria" title="Lighthouse of Alexandria">Lighthouse of Alexandria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Both the <i>Book of the Later Han</i> and the <i>Weil&#252;e</i> mention the "flying" <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">pontoon bridge</a> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#39131;&#27211;</span>) over the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> at <a href="/wiki/Zeugma,_Commagene" title="Zeugma, Commagene">Zeugma, Commagene</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Anatolia" title="Roman Anatolia">Roman Anatolia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_web_notes_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_web_notes-39">[38]</a></sup> The <i>Weil&#252;e</i> also listed what it considered the most important dependent <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassal</a> states of the Roman Empire, providing travel directions and estimates for the distances between them (in <a href="/wiki/Li_(unit)" title="Li (unit)">Chinese miles, <i>li</i></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup> Friedrich Hirth (1885) identified the locations and dependent states of Rome named in the <i>Weil&#252;e</i>;  some of his identifications have been disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[note 2]</a></sup> Hirth identified Si-fu (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#27740;&#24489;</span>) as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emesa" title="Emesa">Emesa</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> John E. Hill (2004) uses linguistic and situational evidence to argue it was <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nabataean_Kingdom" title="Nabataean Kingdom">Nabataean Kingdom</a>, which was annexed by Rome in 106 AD during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_web_notes_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_web_notes-39">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Book_of_Tang" title="New Book of Tang">New Book of Tang</a></i> record that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">the Arabs</a> (<i>Da shi</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#22823;&#39135;</span>) sent their commander Mo-yi (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#25705;&#25341;</span>, <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i>M&#243;y&#232;</i>, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah I</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Syria#Medieval_era" title="History of Syria">governor of Syria</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad caliph</a>, r. 661&#8211;680 AD) to <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674%E2%80%93678)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674&#8211;678)">besiege the Byzantine capital</a>, Constantinople, and forced the Byzantines to pay them tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> The same books also described Constantinople in some detail as having <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Constantinople" title="Walls of Constantinople">strong granite walls</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">water clock</a> mounted with a golden statue of man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_pp46-48_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_pp46-48-41">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Yule" title="Henry Yule">Henry Yule</a> noted that the name of the Byzantine negotiator "Yenyo" (the patrician <a href="/wiki/John_Pitzigaudes" title="John Pitzigaudes">John Pitzigaudes</a>) was mentioned in Chinese sources, an envoy who was unnamed in <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a>\'s account of the man sent to <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> to hold a parley with the Umayyads, followed a few years later by the increase of tributary demands on the Byzantines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> The <i>New Book of Tang</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Wenxian_Tongkao" title="Wenxian Tongkao">Wenxian Tongkao</a></i> described the land of <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> (either the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Aksum</a>) as a desert south-west of the Byzantine Empire that was infested with <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, where the natives had <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">black skin</a> and consumed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palm_date" title="Palm date">Persian dates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-10"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> In discussing the three <a href="/wiki/Islamization_of_the_Sudan_region" title="Islamization of the Sudan region">main religions of Nubia</a> (the Sudan), the <i>Wenxian Tongkao</i> mentions the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Sudan" title="Christianity in Sudan">Daqin religion</a> there and the <a href="/wiki/Sabbath" title="Sabbath">day of rest</a> occurring every seven days for those following the faith of the <i>Da shi</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Sudan" title="Islam in Sudan">Muslim Arabs</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-11"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> It also repeats the claim in the <i>New Book of Tang</i> about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_medicine" title="Roman medicine">Eastern Roman surgical practice</a> of <a href="/wiki/Trepanning" title="Trepanning">trepanning</a> to remove parasites from the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-12"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> The descriptions of Nubia and <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> in the <i>Wenxian Tongkao</i> were ultimately derived from the <i><a href="/wiki/Jingxingji" title="Jingxingji">Jingxingji</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Du_Huan" title="Du Huan">Du Huan</a> (fl. 8th century AD),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> a Chinese travel writer whose text, preserved in the <i><a href="/wiki/Tongdian" title="Tongdian">Tongdian</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Du_You" title="Du You">Du You</a>, is perhaps <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_historiography" title="Ethiopian historiography">the first Chinese source</a> to describe <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopia</a> (<i>Laobosa</i>), in addition to offering descriptions of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Eritrea" title="History of Eritrea">Eritrea</a> (<i>Molin</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abraham_2015_New_African_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abraham_2015_New_African-45">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Embassies_and_travel">Embassies and travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Embassies and travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_imperial_China" title="Foreign relations of imperial China">Foreign relations of imperial China</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Roman Empire">History of the Roman Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UrumqiWarrior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="445" decoding="async" height="382" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/170px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/255px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/340px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UrumqiWarrior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Sampul_tapestry" title="Sampul tapestry">Sampul tapestry</a>, a woollen wall hanging from <a href="/wiki/Lop_County" title="Lop County">Lop County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, China, showing a possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Greek soldier</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian kingdom">Greco-Bactrian kingdom</a> (250&#8211;125 BC), with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_eyes" title="Blue eyes">blue eyes</a>, wielding a spear, and wearing what appears to be a <a href="/wiki/Diadem" title="Diadem">diadem</a> headband; depicted above him is a <a href="/wiki/Centaur" title="Centaur">centaur</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, a common <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motif</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic art</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinjiang_Region_Museum" title="Xinjiang Region Museum">Xinjiang Region Museum</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude">Prelude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Prelude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some contact may have occurred between <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic Greeks</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> in the late 3rd century BC, following the Central Asian campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>, and the establishment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic kingdoms</a> relatively close to China, such as the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a>. Excavations at <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_the_First_Qin_Emperor" title="Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor">the burial site</a> of China\'s first Emperor <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a> (r. 221&#8211;210 BC) suggest <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Greek stylistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_technology" title="Ancient Greek technology">technological</a> influences in the artworks found there, including the famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terracotta_army" title="Terracotta army">terracotta army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> Cultural exchanges at such an early date are generally regarded as conjectural in academia, but excavations of a 4th-century BC tomb in Gansu province belonging to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Qin" title="State of Qin">state of Qin</a> have yielded Western items such as glass beads and a blue-glazed (possibly <a href="/wiki/Faience" title="Faience">faience</a>) beaker of Mediterranean origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> Trade and diplomatic relations between China\'s Han Empire and remnants of Hellenistic Greek civilization under the rule of the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Da Yuezhi</a> began with the Central Asian journeys of the Han envoy <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a> (d. 113 BC). He brought back reports to the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu of Han</a> about the "<a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">Dayuan</a>" in the <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Eschate" title="Alexandria Eschate">Alexandria Eschate</a> as its capital, and the "<a href="/wiki/Daxia" title="Daxia">Daxia</a>" of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, in what is now Afghanistan and Tajikistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> The only well-known Roman traveller to have visited the easternmost fringes of Central Asia was <a href="/wiki/Maes_Titianus" title="Maes Titianus">Maes Titianus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[note 3]</a></sup> a contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> in either the late 1st or early 2nd century AD<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[note 4]</a></sup> who visited a "Stone Tower" that has been identified by historians as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tashkurgan_Town" title="Tashkurgan Town">Tashkurgan</a> in the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pamirs" title="Pamirs">Pamirs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[note 5]</a></sup> or a similar monument in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alai_Valley" title="Alai Valley">Alai Valley</a> just west of <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a>, Xinjiang, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Embassy_to_Augustus">Embassy to Augustus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Embassy to Augustus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The historian Florus described the visit of numerous envoys, including the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seres" title="Seres">Seres</a>" (possibly the Chinese) to the court of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> (r. 27 BC &#8211; 14 AD):\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Even the rest of the nations of the world which were not subject to the imperial sway were sensible of its grandeur, and looked with reverence to the Roman people, the great conqueror of nations. Thus even <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> sent envoys to seek the friendship of Rome. Nay, the Seres came likewise, and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_kingdoms_of_India" title="Middle kingdoms of India">Indians</a> who dwelt beneath the vertical sun, bringing presents of precious stones and pearls and elephants, but thinking all of less moment than the vastness of the journey which they had undertaken, and which they said had occupied four years. In truth it needed but to look at their complexion to see that they were people of another world than ours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In the entire corpus of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_literature" title="Roman literature">Roman literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_historiography" title="Roman historiography">historiography</a>, Yule was unable to uncover any other mention of such a direct diplomatic encounter between the Romans and the Seres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p18_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p18-58">[note 6]</a></sup> He speculated that these people were more likely to have been private merchants than diplomats, since Chinese records insist that <a href="/wiki/Gan_Ying" title="Gan Ying">Gan Ying</a> was the first Chinese to reach as far west as Tiaozhi (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#26465;&#25903;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>) in 97 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p18_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p18-58">[note 6]</a></sup> Yule notes that the 1st-century AD <i>Periplus</i> mentioned that people of <i>Thinae</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinae" title="Sinae">Sinae</a>) were rarely seen, because of the difficulties of reaching that country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoff_2004_paragraph64_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-schoff_2004_paragraph64-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_footnote2_p43_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_footnote2_p43-13">[13]</a></sup> It states that their country, located under <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Minor" title="Ursa Minor">Ursa Minor</a> and on the farthest unknown reaches of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, was the origin of raw silk and fine silk cloth that was traded overland from Bactria to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barygaza" title="Barygaza">Barygaza</a>, as well as down the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoff_2004_paragraph64_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-schoff_2004_paragraph64-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Envoy_Gan_Ying">Envoy Gan Ying</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Envoy Gan Ying">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1356" data-file-width="906" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg/170px-Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg/255px-Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg/340px-Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Daqin_in_Sancai_Tuhui.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Chinese impression of the Daqin people, from the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming-dynasty</a> encyclopedia <i><a href="/wiki/Sancai_Tuhui" title="Sancai Tuhui">Sancai Tuhui</a></i>, 1609</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_the_Western_Regions" title="Protectorate of the Western Regions">Protectorate of the Western Regions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_exploration" title="Chinese exploration">Chinese exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">Dayuan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daxia" title="Daxia">Daxia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kangju" title="Kangju">Kangju</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">History of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> general <a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a> (32&#8211;102 AD), in a series of military successes which brought the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> of Xinjiang) back under Chinese control and suzerainty, defeated the Da Yuezhi in 90 AD and the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Northern Xiongnu</a> in 91 AD, forcing the submission of city-states such as <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a> (Indo-European <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> settlements, respectively),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup> and finally <a href="/wiki/Karasahr" title="Karasahr">Karasahr</a> in 94 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup> An embassy from the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> had earlier arrived at the Han court in 89 AD and, while Ban was stationed with his army in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Khotan" title="Kingdom of Khotan">Khotan</a>, another Parthian embassy came in 101 AD, this time bringing exotic gifts such as <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostriches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crespigny_2007_pp590-591_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-crespigny_2007_pp590-591-62">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 97 AD, Ban Chao sent an envoy named Gan Ying to explore the far west. Gan made his way from the Tarim Basin to <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> and reached the Persian Gulf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crespigny_2007_pp_239-240_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-crespigny_2007_pp_239-240-63">[57]</a></sup> Gan left a detailed account of western countries; he apparently reached as far as Mesopotamia, then under the control of the Parthian Empire. He intended to sail to the Roman Empire, but was discouraged when told that the trip was dangerous and could take two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup> Deterred, he returned to China bringing much new information on the countries to the west of Chinese-controlled territories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup> as far as the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crespigny_2007_pp_239-240_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-crespigny_2007_pp_239-240-63">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gan Ying is thought to have left an account of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> (Daqin in Chinese) which relied on secondary sources&#8212;likely sailors in the ports which he visited. The <i>Book of the Later Han</i> locates it in <i>Haixi</i> ("west of the sea", or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-13"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-john_hill_houhanshu_2003_web_translation_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-john_hill_houhanshu_2003_web_translation-67">[61]</a></sup> the sea is the one known to the Greeks and Romans as the <a href="/wiki/Erythraean_Sea" title="Erythraean Sea">Erythraean Sea</a>, which included the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>, and Red Sea):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2009_23_25_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2009_23_25-68">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Its territory extends for several thousands of li  [a <i>li</i> during the Han dynasty equalled 415.8 metres].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[63]</a></sup> They have established postal relays at intervals, which are all plastered and whitewashed. There are pines and cypresses, as well as trees and plants of all kinds.  It has more than four hundred walled towns. There are several tens of smaller dependent kingdoms. The walls of the towns are made of stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The <i>Book of the Later Han</i> gives a positive, if inaccurate, view of <a href="/wiki/Cursus_honorum" title="Cursus honorum">Roman governance</a>:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Their kings are not permanent rulers, but they appoint men of merit. When a severe calamity visits the country, or untimely rain-storms, the king is deposed and replaced by another. The one relieved from his duties submits to his degradation without a murmur. The inhabitants of that country are tall and well-proportioned, somewhat like the Han [Chinese], whence they are called [Daqin].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[65]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Yule noted that although the description of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Constitution" title="Roman Constitution">Roman Constitution</a> and products was garbled, the <i>Book of the Later Han</i> offered an accurate depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef_fish" title="Coral reef fish">coral fisheries in the Mediterranean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_pp43-44_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_pp43-44-72">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">Coral</a> was a highly valued luxury item in Han China, imported among other items from India (mostly overland and perhaps also by sea), the latter region being where the Romans sold coral and obtained <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[67]</a></sup> The original list of Roman products given in the <i>Book of the Later Han</i>, such as <a href="/wiki/Sea_silk" title="Sea silk">sea silk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinnabar" title="Cinnabar">cinnabar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> cloth, is expanded in the <i><a href="/wiki/Weil%C3%BCe" title="Weil&#252;e">Weil&#252;e</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[68]</a></sup> The <i>Weil&#252;e</i> also claimed that in 134 AD the ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Shule_Kingdom" title="Shule Kingdom">Shule Kingdom</a> (Kashgar), who had been a hostage at the court of the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>, offered blue (or green) gems originating from <i>Haixi</i> as gifts to the Eastern Han court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fan_Ye_(historian)" title="Fan Ye (historian)">Fan Ye</a>, the editor of the <i>Book of the Later Han</i>, wrote that former generations of Chinese had never reached these far western regions, but that the report of Gan Ying revealed to the Chinese their lands, customs and products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[69]</a></sup> The <i>Book of the Later Han</i> also asserts that the Parthians (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: &#23433;&#24687;; <i>Anxi</i>) wished "to control the trade in multi-coloured Chinese silks" and therefore intentionally blocked the Romans from reaching China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-john_hill_houhanshu_2003_web_translation_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-john_hill_houhanshu_2003_web_translation-67">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_Roman_Greeks_in_Burma_and_China">Possible Roman Greeks in Burma and China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Possible Roman Greeks in Burma and China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1770" data-file-width="2364" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg/220px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg/330px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg/440px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_056.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> ceramic vessels showing acrobats balancing by hand on their rims; the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Shiji</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Book of Han</a></i> state that <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_II_of_Parthia" title="Mithridates II of Parthia">Mithridates II</a> of the Parthian Empire sent gifts including Syrian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juggler" title="Juggler">jugglers</a> to the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu of Han</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-14"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> the <i>Book of the Later Han</i> states that a king of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Myanmar" title="History of Myanmar">Burma</a> sent acrobats originally from Daqin to the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_An_of_Han" title="Emperor An of Han">Emperor An of Han</a> in 120 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_of_later_han_passage_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-book_of_later_han_passage-76">[note 7]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>It is possible that a group of Greek acrobatic performers, who claimed to be from a place "west of the seas" (Roman Egypt, which the <i>Book of the Later Han</i> related to the Daqin empire), were presented <a href="/wiki/Pyu_city-states" title="Pyu city-states">by a king of Burma</a> to Emperor An of Han in 120 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_of_later_han_passage_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-book_of_later_han_passage-76">[note 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[71]</a></sup> It is known that in both the Parthian Empire and Kushan Empire of Asia, ethnic Greeks continued to be employed after the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> as musicians and athletes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[73]</a></sup> The <i>Book of the Later Han</i> states that Emperor An transferred these entertainers from his countryside residence to the capital <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, where they gave a performance at his court and were rewarded with gold, silver, and other gifts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[74]</a></sup> Raoul McLaughlin notes that the Romans knew Burma as <i><a href="/wiki/Greater_India#Medieval_Europe" title="Greater India">India Trans Gangem</a></i> (India Beyond the Ganges) and that <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> listed the cities of Burma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2010_p58_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2010_p58-82">[75]</a></sup> McLaughlin speculates that the Romans <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">were selling slaves</a> to the Burmese and that this is how the entertainers originally reached Burma before they were sent by the Burmese ruler to Emperor An in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2010_p58_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2010_p58-82">[75]</a></sup> Syrian jugglers were renowned in Western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_literature" title="Classical literature">Classical literature</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[76]</a></sup> and Chinese sources from the 2nd century BC to the 2nd century AD mention them as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p152_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p152-84">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Roman_embassy">First Roman embassy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: First Roman embassy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dahuting_mural,_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dahuting_mural%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg/220px-Dahuting_mural%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dahuting_mural%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg/330px-Dahuting_mural%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dahuting_mural%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg/440px-Dahuting_mural%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dahuting_mural,_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mural showing women dressed in traditional <i><a href="/wiki/Hanfu" title="Hanfu">Hanfu</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> robes, <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Dahuting_Tomb_murals" title="c:Category:Dahuting Tomb murals">from the Dahuting Tomb</a> (Chinese: <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#25171;&#34382;&#20141;&#27721;&#22675;</span>, pinyin: <i>Dahuting Han mu</i>) of the late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_dynasty" title="Eastern Han dynasty">Eastern Han dynasty</a> (25&#8211;220 AD), located in <a href="/wiki/Zhengzhou" title="Zhengzhou">Zhengzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> province, China</div></div></div>\n<p>The first group of people claiming to be an ambassadorial mission of Romans to China was recorded as having arrived in 166 AD by the <i>Book of the Later Han</i>.
