The embassy came to <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huan_of_Han" title="Emperor Huan of Han">Emperor Huan</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_China" title="Han China">Han China</a> from "Andun" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">&#23433;&#25958;</span>; Emperor <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> or <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius Antoninus</a>), "king of Daqin" (Rome).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_crespigny_2007_600_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_crespigny_2007_600-85">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[79]</a></sup> As Antoninus Pius died in 161 AD, leaving the empire to his adoptive son Marcus Aurelius Antoninus, and the envoy arrived in 166 AD, confusion remains about who sent the mission, as both emperors were named "Antoninus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pulleyblank_1999,_p._78_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pulleyblank_1999,_p._78-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_2009,_p._27_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_2009,_p._27-87">[80]</a></sup> The Roman mission came from the south (therefore probably by sea), entering China by the frontier of <a href="/wiki/Rinan" title="Rinan">Rinan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tonkin" title="Tonkin">Tonkin</a> (present-day Vietnam). It brought presents of <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros#Horn_trade_and_use" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceros horns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tortoise" title="Tortoise">tortoise</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_shell" title="Animal shell">shell</a>, probably acquired in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Asia" title="Southern Asia">Southern Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_2009,_p._27_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_2009,_p._27-87">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[81]</a></sup> The text states that it was the first time there had been direct contact between the two countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_2009,_p._27_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_2009,_p._27-87">[80]</a></sup> Yule speculated that the Roman visitors must have lost their original wares due to robbery or shipwreck and used the gifts instead, prompting Chinese sources to suspect them of withholding their more precious valuables, which Yule notes was the same criticism directed at <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Beijing" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Beijing">papal missionary</a> <a href="/wiki/John_of_Montecorvino" title="John of Montecorvino">John of Montecorvino</a> when he arrived in China in the late 13th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[82]</a></sup> Historians <a href="/wiki/Rafe_de_Crespigny" title="Rafe de Crespigny">Rafe de Crespigny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Fibiger_Bang" title="Peter Fibiger Bang">Peter Fibiger Bang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Ball" title="Warwick Ball">Warwick Ball</a> believe that this was most likely a group of <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">Roman merchants</a> rather than official diplomats sent by Marcus Aurelius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p152_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p152-84">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_crespigny_2007_600_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-de_crespigny_2007_600-85">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[83]</a></sup> Crespigny stresses that the presence of this Roman embassy as well as others from <a href="/wiki/Tianzhu_(India)" title="Tianzhu (India)">Tianzhu</a> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_India" title="Northern India">northern India</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Buyeo" title="Buyeo">Buyeo</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>) provided much-needed prestige for Emperor Huan, as he was facing serious <a href="/wiki/Disasters_of_the_Partisan_Prohibitions" title="Disasters of the Partisan Prohibitions">political troubles and fallout</a> for the forced suicide of politician <a href="/wiki/Liang_Ji" title="Liang Ji">Liang Ji</a>, who had dominated the Han government well after the death of his sister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Liang_Na" title="Empress Liang Na">Empress Liang Na</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[84]</a></sup> Yule emphasised that the Roman embassy was said to come by way of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> in northern Vietnam, the same route that Chinese sources claimed the embassies from Tianzhu (northern India) had used in 159 and 161 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Roman_embassies">Other Roman embassies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other Roman embassies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb,_Guixian,_China.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg/220px-Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg/330px-Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg/440px-Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb,_Guixian,_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Green <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glass</a> cup unearthed from an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_dynasty" title="Eastern Han dynasty">Eastern Han dynasty</a> (25&#8211;220 AD) tomb of <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> (bordering modern-day Vietnam in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_China" title="Southern China">southern China</a>)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="718" data-file-width="898" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg/220px-Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg/330px-Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg/440px-Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Eastern Roman</a> embassy to <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a> in 643 AD</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Weil%C3%BCe" title="Weil&#252;e">Weil&#252;e</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Liang" title="Book of Liang">Book of Liang</a></i> record the arrival in 226 AD of a merchant <a href="/wiki/Daqin#Naming_conventions" title="Daqin">named Qin Lun</a> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#31206;&#35542;</span>) from <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Roman Empire">the Roman Empire</a> (Daqin) at Jiaozhi (Chinese-controlled northern Vietnam).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p152_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p152-84">[77]</a></sup> Wu Miao, the Prefect of Jiaozhi, sent him to the court of <a href="/wiki/Sun_Quan" title="Sun Quan">Sun Quan</a> (the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Wu" title="Eastern Wu">Eastern Wu</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p152_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p152-84">[77]</a></sup> where Sun requested that he provide him with a report on his native country and its people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-15"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-5"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup> An expedition was mounted to return the merchant along with ten female and ten male "blackish coloured dwarfs" he had requested as a curiosity, as well as a Chinese officer, Liu Xian of Huiji (in <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>), who died en route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-16"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-6"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[86]</a></sup> According to the <i>Weil&#252;e</i> and <i>Book of Liang</i> Roman merchants were active in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" title="Kingdom of Funan">Cambodia and Vietnam</a>, a claim supported by modern archaeological finds of ancient Mediterranean goods in the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asian</a> countries of Vietnam, Thailand, Malaysia, and Indonesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-17"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-7"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Yule mentions that in the early 3rd century AD a ruler of Daqin sent an envoy with gifts to the northern Chinese court of <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Cao Wei</a> (220&#8211;265 AD) that included <a href="/wiki/Dichroic_glass" title="Dichroic glass">glassware of various colours</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p53_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p53-94">[87]</a></sup> Several years later a Daqin craftsman is mentioned as showing the Chinese how to make "<a href="/wiki/Glassblowing" title="Glassblowing">flints into crystal by means of fire</a>", a curiosity to the Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_p53_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_p53-94">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another embassy from Daqin is recorded as bringing tributary gifts to the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Jin" title="Western Jin">Jin Empire</a> (265&#8211;420 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p152_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p152-84">[77]</a></sup> This occurred in 284 AD during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Jin" title="Emperor Wu of Jin">Emperor Wu of Jin</a> (r. 266&#8211;290 AD), and was recorded in the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jin" title="Book of Jin">Book of Jin</a></i>, as well as the later <i><a href="/wiki/Wenxian_Tongkao" title="Wenxian Tongkao">Wenxian Tongkao</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-18"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p152_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p152-84">[77]</a></sup> This embassy was presumably sent by the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Carus" title="Carus">Carus</a> (r. 282&#8211;283 AD), whose brief reign was <a href="/wiki/Bahram_II" title="Bahram II">preoccupied by war</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Persia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_pp53-54_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_pp53-54-95">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fulin:_Eastern_Roman_embassies">Fulin: Eastern Roman embassies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fulin: Eastern Roman embassies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">Europeans in Medieval China</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg"><img alt="Painting of three men in respectful posture approaching a man seated on a palanquin and surrounded by attendants on the left" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1049" data-file-width="1890" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg/220px-Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg/330px-Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg/440px-Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a> (r. 626&#8211;649 AD) receives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gar_Tongtsen_Y%C3%BClsung" title="Gar Tongtsen Y&#252;lsung">Gar Tongtsen Y&#252;lsung</a>, ambassador of the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a>, at his court. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_Receiving_the_Tibetan_Envoy" title="Emperor Taizong Receiving the Tibetan Envoy">Later copy</a> of a 641 painting by the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty_art" title="Tang dynasty art">Tang court artist</a> <a href="/wiki/Yan_Liben" title="Yan Liben">Yan Liben</a> (600&#8211;673 AD).</div></div></div>\n<p>Chinese histories for the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618&#8211;907 AD) record contacts with merchants from "Fulin" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#25282;&#33787;</span>), the new name used to designate the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-19"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkinson_2000_footnote14_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson_2000_footnote14-96">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[90]</a></sup> The first reported diplomatic contact took place in 643 AD during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Constans_II" title="Constans II">Constans II</a> (641&#8211;668 AD) and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a> (626&#8211;649 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-20"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i>, followed by the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Book_of_Tang" title="New Book of Tang">New Book of Tang</a></i>, provides the name "Po-to-li" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#27874;&#22810;&#21147;</span>, <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i>B&#333;du&#333;l&#236;</i>) for Constans II, which Hirth conjectured to be a transliteration of <i>K&#333;nstantinos Pogonatos</i>, or "Constantine the Bearded", giving him the title of a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">king</a> (&#29579; <i>w&#225;ng</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-21"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> Yule<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[91]</a></sup> and S. A. M. Adshead offer a different transliteration stemming from "<a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a>", possibly a reference to one of the acting <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regents</a> for the 13-year-old Byzantine monarch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adshead_1995_p105_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-adshead_1995_p105-99">[92]</a></sup> The Tang histories record that Constans II sent an embassy in the 17th year of the Zhenguan (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#35998;&#35264;</span>) regnal period (643 AD), bearing gifts of <a href="/wiki/Cranberry_glass" title="Cranberry glass">red glass</a> and green <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-22"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> Yule points out that <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_III" title="Yazdegerd III">Yazdegerd III</a> (r. 632&#8211;651 AD), last ruler of the Sasanian Empire, sent diplomats to China to secure aid from Emperor Taizong (<a href="/wiki/Protectorate_General_to_Pacify_the_West" title="Protectorate General to Pacify the West">considered the suzerain</a> over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferghana" title="Ferghana">Ferghana</a> in Central Asia) during the loss of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian heartland</a> to the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, which may also have prompted the Byzantines to send envoys to China amid their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Levant" title="Muslim conquest of the Levant">recent loss of Syria to the Muslims</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[93]</a></sup> Tang Chinese sources also recorded how Sasanian prince <a href="/wiki/Peroz_III" title="Peroz III">Peroz III</a> (636&#8211;679 AD) fled to Tang China following the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">conquest of Persia by the growing Islamic caliphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adshead_1995_p105_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-adshead_1995_p105-99">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Yule asserts that the additional Fulin embassies during the Tang period arrived in 711 and 719 AD, with another in 742 AD that may have been Nestorian monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[95]</a></sup> Adshead lists four official diplomatic contacts with Fulin in the <i>Old Book of Tang</i> as occurring in 643, 667, 701, and 719 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[96]</a></sup> He speculates that the absence of these missions in Western literary sources can be explained by how the Byzantines typically viewed political relations with powers of the East, as well as the possibility that they were launched on behalf of frontier officials instead of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_bureaucracy_and_aristocracy" title="Byzantine bureaucracy and aristocracy">the central government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adshead_1995_p104_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-adshead_1995_p104-104">[97]</a></sup> Yule and Adshead concur that a Fulin diplomatic mission occurred during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_II" title="Justinian II">Justinian II</a> (r. 685&#8211;695 AD; 705&#8211;711 AD). Yule claims it occurred in the year of the emperor\'s death, 711 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[98]</a></sup> whereas Adshead contends that it took place in 701 AD during the usurpation of <a href="/wiki/Leontios" title="Leontios">Leontios</a> and the emperor\'s exile in <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>, perhaps the reason for its omission in <a href="/wiki/Roman_historiography" title="Roman historiography">Byzantine records</a> and the source for confusion in Chinese histories about precisely who sent this embassy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adshead_1995_pp105-106_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-adshead_1995_pp105-106-106">[99]</a></sup> Justinian II regained the throne with the aid of <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a> and a marriage alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Khazars" title="Khazars">Khazars</a>. Adshead therefore believes a mission sent to Tang China would be consistent with Justinian II\'s behaviour, especially if he had knowledge of the permission <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Wu_Zetian" title="Empress Wu Zetian">Empress Wu Zetian</a> granted to <a href="/wiki/Narsieh" title="Narsieh">Narsieh</a>, son of Peroz III, to march against the Arabs in Central Asia at the end of the 7th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adshead_1995_pp105-106_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-adshead_1995_pp105-106-106">[99]</a></sup> The 719 AD Fulin embassy ostensibly came from <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III the Isaurian</a> (r. 717&#8211;741 AD) to the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong of Tang</a> (r. 712&#8211;756 AD), during a time when the Byzantine emperor was again reaching out to Eastern powers with a renewed Khazar marriage alliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adshead_1995_p106_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-adshead_1995_p106-107">[100]</a></sup> The year of this embassy coincided with Xuanzong\'s refusal to provide aid to the <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdians</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana" title="Muslim conquest of Transoxiana">Arab invasion force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adshead_1995_p106_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-adshead_1995_p106-107">[100]</a></sup> An embassy from the Umayyad Caliphate was received by the Tang court in 732 AD; the Arab victory at the 751 AD <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talas" title="Battle of Talas">Battle of Talas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a> crippled Tang Chinese interventionist efforts in Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The last diplomatic contacts with Fulin are recorded as having taken place in the 11th century AD. From the <i>Wenxian Tongkao</i>, written by historian <a href="/wiki/Ma_Duanlin" title="Ma Duanlin">Ma Duanlin</a> (1245&#8211;1322), and from the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Song" title="History of Song">History of Song</a></i>, it is known that the Byzantine emperor <a href="/wiki/Michael_VII_Doukas" title="Michael VII Doukas">Michael VII Parapinak&#275;s Caesar</a> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#28357;&#21147;&#27801;&#38728;&#25913;&#25746;</span>, <i>Mie li sha ling kai sa</i>) of Fulin sent an embassy to China\'s <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> that arrived in 1081 AD, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Shenzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Shenzong of Song">Emperor Shenzong of Song</a> (r. 1067&#8211;1085 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-23"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SezginEhrig-Eggert1996_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-SezginEhrig-Eggert1996-109">[102]</a></sup> The <i>History of Song</i> described the tributary gifts given by the Byzantine embassy as well as the products made in Byzantium. It also described punishments used in <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_law" title="Byzantine law">Byzantine law</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">capital punishment</a> of being stuffed into a "feather bag" and thrown into the sea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-24"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> probably the Romano-Byzantine practice of <i><a href="/wiki/Poena_cullei" title="Poena cullei">poena cullei</a></i> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> \'penalty of the sack\').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[103]</a></sup> The final recorded embassy arrived in 1091 AD, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I Komnenos</a> (r. 1081&#8211;1118 AD); this event is only mentioned in passing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Yuan" title="History of Yuan">History of Yuan</a></i> offers a biography of a Byzantine man named Ai-sie (transliteration of either Joshua or Joseph), who originally served the court of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_Khan" title="G&#252;y&#252;k Khan">G&#252;y&#252;k Khan</a> but later became a head <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_astronomy" title="Byzantine astronomy">astronomer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_medicine" title="Byzantine medicine">physician</a> for the court of <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, the Mongol founder of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> (1271&#8211;1368 AD), at <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bretschneider_1888p144_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-bretschneider_1888p144-112">[105]</a></sup> He was eventually granted the title Prince of Fulin (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#25282;&#33787;&#29579;</span>, <i>F&#250;l&#464;n w&#225;ng</i>) and his children were listed with their <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese names</a>, which seem to match with transliterations of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_name" title="Christian name">Christian names</a> Elias, Luke, and Antony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bretschneider_1888p144_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-bretschneider_1888p144-112">[105]</a></sup> Kublai Khan is also known to have sent Nestorian monks, including <a href="/wiki/Rabban_Bar_Sauma" title="Rabban Bar Sauma">Rabban Bar Sauma</a>, to the court of Byzantine ruler <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_II_Palaiologos" title="Andronikos II Palaiologos">Andronikos II Palaiologos</a> (r. 1282&#8211;1328 AD), whose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine-Mongol_alliance" title="Byzantine-Mongol alliance">half-sisters were married to the great-grandsons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>, making this Byzantine ruler an <a href="/wiki/Affinity_(law)" title="Affinity (law)">in-law with the Mongol ruler</a> in Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/140px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/210px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/280px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Western_Han_blue_glass_bowl.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="381" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Western_Han_blue_glass_bowl.JPG/160px-Western_Han_blue_glass_bowl.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Western_Han_blue_glass_bowl.JPG/240px-Western_Han_blue_glass_bowl.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Western_Han_blue_glass_bowl.JPG/320px-Western_Han_blue_glass_bowl.JPG 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><i>Left</i>: Woven <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> textile from <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> province, China, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> period, 2nd century BC<br/> <i>Right</i>: A Western Han (202 BC &#8211; 9 AD) blue-glass bowl; the Chinese had been making glass beads based on imports from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> (722&#8211;481 BC), and the first Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glassware" title="Glassware">glassware</a> appeared during the Western Han era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[107]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Within the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Mongol conquest of the Song dynasty">eventually included all of China</a>, there were enough Westerners travelling there that in 1340 AD <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Balducci_Pegolotti" title="Francesco Balducci Pegolotti">Francesco Balducci Pegolotti</a> compiled a <a href="/wiki/Guide_book" title="Guide book">guide book</a> for fellow merchants on how to exchange silver for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_money" title="Paper money">paper money</a> to purchase silk <a href="/wiki/History_of_Beijing" title="History of Beijing">in Khanbaliq (Beijing)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[108]</a></sup> By this stage the Eastern Roman Empire, temporarily dismantled by the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>, had shrunk to the size of a rump state in <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greece" title="Byzantine Greece">parts of Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Anatolia" title="Byzantine Anatolia">Anatolia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ma_Duanlin" title="Ma Duanlin">Ma Duanlin</a>, author of the <i>Wenxian Tongkao</i>, noted the shifting political boundaries, albeit based on generally inaccurate and distorted <a href="/wiki/Political_geography" title="Political geography">political geography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-25"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> He wrote that historians of the Tang Dynasty considered "Daqin" and "Fulin" to be the same country, but he had his reservations about this due to discrepancies in geographical accounts and other concerns (<a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a> spelling):\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>During the sixth year of Yuan-yu [1091] they sent two embassies, and their king was presented, by Imperial order, with 200 pieces of cloth, pairs of silver vases, and clothing with gold bound in a girdle. According to the historians of the T\'ang dynasty, the country of Fulin was held to be identical with the ancient Ta-ts\'in. It should be remarked, however, that, although Ta-ts\'in has from the Later Han dynasty when Zhongguo was first communicated with, till down to the Chin and T\'ang dynasties has offered tribute without interruption, yet the historians of the "four reigns" of the Sung dynasty, in their notices of Fulin, hold that this country has not sent tribute to court up to the time of Yuan-feng [1078&#8211;1086] when they sent their first embassy offering local produce. If we, now, hold together the two accounts of Fulin as transmitted by the two different historians, we find that, in the account of the T\'ang dynasty, this country is said "to border on the great sea in the west"; whereas the Sung account says that "in the west you have still thirty days\' journey to the sea;" and the remaining boundaries do also not tally in the two accounts; nor do the products and the customs of the people. I suspect that we have before us merely an accidental similarity of the name, and that the country is indeed not identical with Ta-ts\'in. I have, for this reason, appended the Fulin account of the T\'ang dynasty to my chapter on Ta-ts\'in, and represented this Fulin of the Sung dynasty as a separate country altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[111]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a></i> expounds how the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> (1368&#8211;1644 AD), sent a merchant of Fulin named "Nieh-ku-lun" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#25423;&#21476;&#20523;</span>) back to his native country with a letter announcing the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Ming_dynasty" title="History of the Ming dynasty">founding of the Ming dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-26"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grant2005_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2005-119">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirth1885_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirth1885-120">[113]</a></sup> It is speculated that the merchant was a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Beijing" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Beijing">former archbishop</a> of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Beijing" title="History of Beijing">Khanbaliq</a> called Nicolaus de Bentra (who succeeded <a href="/wiki/John_of_Montecorvino" title="John of Montecorvino">John of Montecorvino</a> for that position).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-27"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luttwak2009_170_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luttwak2009_170-121">[114]</a></sup> The <i>History of Ming</i> goes on to explain that contacts between China and Fulin ceased after this point and an envoy of the great western sea (the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>) did not appear in China again until the 16th century AD, with the 1582 AD arrival of the Italian <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuit missionary</a> <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> in Portuguese <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-28"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[note 8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_relations">Trade relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Trade relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_exports_to_China">Roman exports to China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Roman exports to China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">Indo-Roman trade relations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg/220px-Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg/330px-Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg/440px-Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Verre_gallo-romain_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman glass from the 2nd century AD</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Menade.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Menade.jpg/170px-Menade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Menade.jpg/255px-Menade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Menade.jpg/340px-Menade.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Menade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman fresco from <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> showing a <a href="/wiki/Maenad" title="Maenad">Maenad</a> in silk dress, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naples_National_Archaeological_Museum" title="Naples National Archaeological Museum">Naples National Archaeological Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain,_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1654" data-file-width="1074" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain%2C_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg/170px-Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain%2C_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain%2C_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg/255px-Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain%2C_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain%2C_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg/340px-Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain%2C_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nizami_Ganjavi_-_Separated_by_a_Green_Curtain,_a_Chinese_and_Roman_Artist_Compete_with_Each_Other_-_Walters_W604261A_-_Full_Page.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting entitled <i>Separated by a Green Curtain, a Chinese and Roman Artist Compete with Each Other</i>, original by <a href="/wiki/Nizami_Ganjavi" title="Nizami Ganjavi">Nizami Ganjavi</a> (1141&#8211;1209), from a Persian <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid</a>-era copy dated 1481</div></div></div>\n<p>Direct trade links between the Mediterranean lands and India had been established in the late 2nd century BC by the Hellenistic <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup> Greek navigators learned to use the regular pattern of the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds for their trade voyages in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>. The lively sea trade in Roman times is confirmed by the excavation of large deposits of Roman coins along much of the coast of India. Many trading ports with links to Roman communities have been <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">identified in India</a> and Sri Lanka along the route used by the Roman mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[116]</a></sup> Archaeological evidence stretching from the Red Sea ports of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a> to India suggests that <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">Roman commercial</a> activity in the Indian Ocean and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Southeast_Asia" title="History of Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> declined heavily with the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">Antonine Plague</a> of 166 AD, the same year as the first Roman embassy to Han China, where similar plague outbreaks had occurred from 151 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2010_p59-60_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2010_p59-60-126">[118]</a></sup>\n</p><p>High-quality <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> from Roman manufacturers in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and Syria was exported to many parts of Asia, including Han China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glassware</a> discovered in China is a blue soda-lime glass bowl dating to the early 1st century BC and excavated from a Western Han tomb in the southern port city of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, which may have come there via the Indian Ocean and <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an_2002_p83_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-an_2002_p83-128">[120]</a></sup> Other Roman glass items include a mosaic-glass bowl found in a prince\'s tomb near <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> dated to 67 AD and a glass bottle with opaque white streaks found in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> tomb of <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> Roman and Persian glassware has been found in a 5th-century AD tomb of <a href="/wiki/Gyeongju" title="Gyeongju">Gyeongju</a>, Korea, capital of ancient <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>, east of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup> Roman glass beads have been discovered as far as Japan, within the 5th-century AD <a href="/wiki/Kofun_period" title="Kofun period">Kofun-era</a> Utsukushi burial mound near <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From Chinese sources it is known that other Roman luxury items were esteemed by the Chinese. These include gold-embroidered <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">rugs</a> and gold-coloured cloth, <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> cloth, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_silk" title="Sea silk">sea silk</a>, which was a cloth made from the silk-like hairs of a Mediterranean shell-fish, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pinna_nobilis" title="Pinna nobilis">Pinna nobilis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-29"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thorley_1971_71_80_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thorley_1971_71_80-132">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_2007_115_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis_2007_115-134">[126]</a></sup>  As well as silver and bronze items found throughout China dated to the 3rd&#8211;2nd centuries BC and perhaps originating from the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, there is also a Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilded" title="Gilded">gilded</a> silver plate dated to the 2nd&#8211;3rd centuries AD and found in <a href="/wiki/Jingyuan_County,_Gansu" title="Jingyuan County, Gansu">Jingyuan County, Gansu</a>, with a raised <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a> image in the centre depicting the Greco-Roman god <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a> resting on a feline creature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A maritime route opened up with the Chinese-controlled port of <a href="/wiki/Rinan" title="Rinan">Rinan</a> in Jiaozhi (centred in modern Vietnam) and the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_people" title="Khmer people">Khmer</a> kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" title="Kingdom of Funan">Funan</a> by the 2nd century AD, if not earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborne_2006_pp24-25_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-osborne_2006_pp24-25-136">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[129]</a></sup> Jiaozhi was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Richthofen" title="Ferdinand von Richthofen">Ferdinand von Richthofen</a> in 1877 to have been the port known to the Greco-Roman geographer <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattigara" title="Cattigara">Cattigara</a>, situated near modern <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Ptolemy wrote</a> that Cattigara lay beyond the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Chersonese" title="Golden Chersonese">Golden Chersonese</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>) and was visited by a Greek sailor named Alexander, most likely a merchant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup> Richthofen\'s identification of Cattigara as Hanoi was widely accepted until archaeological discoveries at <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="&#211;c Eo">&#211;c Eo</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City" title="Ho Chi Minh City">Ho Chi Minh City</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Mekong_Delta" title="Mekong Delta">Mekong Delta</a> during the mid-20th century suggested this may have been its location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[note 9]</a></sup> Granville Allen Mawer states that Ptolemy\'s Cattigara seems to correspond with the latitude of modern &#211;c Eo, where Roman goods and native jewellery imitating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonine" title="Antonine">Antonine</a> Roman coins have been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawer_2013_p38_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-mawer_2013_p38-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cattigara_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cattigara-140">[note 10]</a></sup> At &#211;c Eo, which was once located along the coastline, Roman coins were among the vestiges of long-distance trade discovered by the French archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Malleret" title="Louis Malleret">Louis Malleret</a> in the 1940s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborne_2006_pp24-25_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-osborne_2006_pp24-25-136">[128]</a></sup> These include Roman golden <a href="/wiki/Medal" title="Medal">medallions</a> from the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> and his successor Marcus Aurelius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oreilly_2007_p97_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-oreilly_2007_p97-141">[131]</a></sup> Ancient Roman glass beads and bracelets were also found at the site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oreilly_2007_p97_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-oreilly_2007_p97-141">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The trade connection from Cattigara extended, via ports on the coasts of India and Sri Lanka, all the way to Roman-controlled ports in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabataean" title="Nabataean">Nabataean</a> territories on the north-eastern coast of the Red Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[132]</a></sup> The archaeologist Warwick Ball does not consider discoveries such as the Roman and Roman-inspired goods at &#211;c Eo, a coin of Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Tonkin" title="Tonkin">Tonkin</a>, and a Roman bronze lamp at P\'ong Tuk in the Mekong Delta, to be conclusive proof that Romans visited these areas and suggests that the items could have been introduced by Indian merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p153_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p153-143">[133]</a></sup> While observing that the Romans had a recognised trading port in Southeast Asia, Dougald O\'Reilly writes that there is little evidence to suggest Cattigara was &#211;c Eo. He argues that the Roman items found there only indicate that the Indian Ocean trade network extended to the ancient Kingdom of Funan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oreilly_2007_p97_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-oreilly_2007_p97-141">[131]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_silk_in_the_Roman_Empire">Asian silk in the Roman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Asian silk in the Roman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">Chinese trade with the Roman Empire</a>, confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> desire for silk, started in the 1st century BC. The Romans knew of <a href="/wiki/Wild_silk" title="Wild silk">wild silk</a> harvested on <a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Cos</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Coa_vestis" title="Coa vestis">coa vestis</a></i>), but they did not at first make the connection with the silk that was produced in the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarikol_kingdom" title="Sarikol kingdom">Sarikol kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[134]</a></sup> There were few direct trade contacts between Romans and Han Chinese, as the rival Parthians and Kushans were each protecting their lucrative role as trade intermediaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1st century BC silk was still a rare commodity in the Roman world; by the 1st century AD this valuable trade item became much more widely available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitfield_1999_p21_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-whitfield_1999_p21-147">[137]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i> (77&#8211;79 AD), <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> lamented the financial drain of coin from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_economy" title="Roman economy">Roman economy</a> to purchase this expensive luxury. He remarked that Rome\'s "womankind" and the purchase of luxury goods from India, Arabia, and the Seres of the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a> cost the empire roughly 100 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesterces" title="Sesterces">sesterces</a> per year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[138]</a></sup> and claimed that journeys were made to the Seres to acquire silk cloth along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_diving" title="Pearl diving">pearl diving</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_2007_115_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis_2007_115-134">[126]</a></sup> Despite the claims by Pliny the Elder about the trade imbalance and quantity of Rome\'s coinage used to purchase silk, Warwick Ball asserts that the Roman purchase of other foreign commodities, particularly <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spices from India</a>, had a much greater impact on the Roman economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-150">[140]</a></sup> In 14 AD the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a> issued an edict prohibiting the wearing of silk by men, but it continued to flow unabated into the Roman world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitfield_1999_p21_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-whitfield_1999_p21-147">[137]</a></sup>  Beyond the economic concerns that the import of silk caused a huge outflow of wealth, silk clothes were also considered to be decadent and immoral by <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Elder" title="Seneca the Elder">Seneca the Elder</a>:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I can see clothes of silk, if materials that do not hide the body, nor even one\'s decency, can be called clothes&#160;... Wretched flocks of maids labour so that the adulteress may be visible through her thin dress, so that her husband has no more acquaintance than any outsider or foreigner with his wife\'s body.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>Seneca the Elder c.&#160;3 BC&#160;&#8211; 65 AD, <i>Excerpta Controversiae</i> 2.7<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[141]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1984" data-file-width="1383" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg/170px-Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg/255px-Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg/340px-Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gladiateur_Begram_Guimet_18117.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman glass, dated 52&#8211;125 AD and found at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Begram" title="Begram">Begram</a>, Afghanistan, a royal city of the Kushan Empire where, according to <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Ball" title="Warwick Ball">Warwick Ball</a>, it was likely on its way to China via the Silk Road along with other glass items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p153-154_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p153-154-152">[142]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="146" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg/440px-Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> (25&#8211;220 AD) <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese tomb mural</a> showing a banquet with men and women wearing traditional <i><a href="/wiki/Hanfu" title="Hanfu">Hanfu</a></i> silk robes, from the Dahuting Tomb (Chinese: &#25171;&#34382;&#20141;&#27721;&#22675;) on the southern bank of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Suihe_River&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Suihe River (page does not exist)">Suihe River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zhengzhou" title="Zhengzhou">Zhengzhou</a>, China</div></div></div>\n<p>Trade items such as spice and silk had to be paid for with Roman gold coinage. There was some demand in China for Roman glass; the Han Chinese also produced glass in certain locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p153-154_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p153-154-152">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitfield_1999_p21_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-whitfield_1999_p21-147">[137]</a></sup> Chinese-produced glassware date back to the Western Han era (202 BC &#8211; 9 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[143]</a></sup> In dealing with foreign states such as the Parthian Empire, the Han Chinese were perhaps more concerned with diplomatically outmaneuvering their chief enemies, the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>, than with establishing trade, since mercantile pursuits and the <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">merchant class</a> were frowned upon by the <a href="/wiki/Scholar-official" title="Scholar-official">gentry</a> who <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Government of the Han dynasty">dominated the Han government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[144]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_and_Byzantine_currency_discovered_in_China">Roman and Byzantine currency discovered in China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Roman and Byzantine currency discovered in China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">Byzantine silk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine-Mongol_alliance" title="Byzantine-Mongol alliance">Byzantine-Mongol alliance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Economy of the Han dynasty">Economy of the Han dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_China_before_1912" title="Economic history of China before 1912">Economic history of China before 1912</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Numismatics" title="Numismatics">Numismatics</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1162" data-file-width="1161" decoding="async" height="220"
