srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/330px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/440px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze coin of <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a> (337&#8211;361 AD), found in <a href="/wiki/Kargilik_Town" title="Kargilik Town">Karghalik</a>, modern China</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:492px;max-width:492px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Textielschat08.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1640" data-file-width="1515" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Textielschat08.jpg/100px-Textielschat08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Textielschat08.jpg/150px-Textielschat08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Textielschat08.jpg/200px-Textielschat08.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:187px;max-width:187px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Textielschat10.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="881" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Textielschat10.jpg/185px-Textielschat10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Textielschat10.jpg/278px-Textielschat10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Textielschat10.jpg/370px-Textielschat10.jpg 2x" width="185"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:197px;max-width:197px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:11th_century_Byzantine_griffins.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/11th_century_Byzantine_griffins.gif/195px-11th_century_Byzantine_griffins.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/11th_century_Byzantine_griffins.gif/293px-11th_century_Byzantine_griffins.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/11th_century_Byzantine_griffins.gif/390px-11th_century_Byzantine_griffins.gif 2x" width="195"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">Byzantine silk</a> textile fragments, from left to right: a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_archer" title="Horse archer">horse archer</a> and a lion, 8th century AD; Byzantine or Spanish woven silk, 9th&#8211;10th century AD; <a href="/wiki/Griffin" title="Griffin">griffins</a> from an embroidered silk robe, 11th century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Smuggling_of_silkworm_eggs_into_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Smuggling of silkworm eggs into the Byzantine Empire">smuggling of silkworm eggs into the Byzantine Empire</a> from China by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Christian" title="Nestorian Christian">Nestorian Christian</a> monks, the 6th-century AD Byzantine historian <a href="/wiki/Menander_Protector" title="Menander Protector">Menander Protector</a> wrote of how the Sogdians attempted to establish a direct trade of Chinese silk with the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. After forming an alliance with the Sasanian Persian ruler <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_I" title="Khosrow I">Khosrow I</a> to defeat the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephthalite_Empire" title="Hephthalite Empire">Hephthalite Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ist%C3%A4mi" title="Ist&#228;mi">Ist&#228;mi</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kt%C3%BCrk" title="G&#246;kt&#252;rk">G&#246;kt&#252;rk</a> ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_Khaganate" title="Turkic Khaganate">Turkic Khaganate</a>, was approached by Sogdian merchants requesting permission to seek an audience with the Sasanian king of kings for the privilege of travelling through Persian territories to trade with the Byzantines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p133_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p133-155">[145]</a></sup> Ist&#228;mi refused the first request, but when he sanctioned the second one and had the Sogdian embassy sent to the Sasanian king, the latter had the members of the embassy killed by poison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p133_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p133-155">[145]</a></sup> Maniakh, a Sogdian diplomat, convinced Ist&#228;mi to send an embassy directly to Byzantium\'s capital <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, which arrived in 568 AD and offered not only silk as a gift to Byzantine ruler <a href="/wiki/Justin_II" title="Justin II">Justin II</a>, but also an alliance against Sasanian Persia. Justin II agreed and sent an embassy under <a href="/wiki/Zemarchus" title="Zemarchus">Zemarchus</a> to the Turkic Khaganate, ensuring the direct silk trade desired by the Sogdians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p133_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p133-155">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[147]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_finds">Archaeological finds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Archaeological finds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The small number of <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_coinage" title="Byzantine coinage">Byzantine coins</a> found during excavations of Central Asian and Chinese archaeological sites from this era suggests that direct trade with the Sogdians remained limited. This was despite the fact that ancient Romans imported Han Chinese silk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[148]</a></sup> and discoveries in contemporary tombs indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Economy of the Han dynasty">Han-dynasty Chinese</a> imported Roman glassware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an_2002_pp79-94_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-an_2002_pp79-94-159">[149]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Valerie_Hansen" title="Valerie Hansen">Valerie Hansen</a> wrote in 2012 that no Roman coins from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> (507&#8211;27 BC) or the <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">Principate</a> (27 BC &#8211; 284 AD) era of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> have been found in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p97_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p97-160">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Ball" title="Warwick Ball">Warwick Ball</a> (2016) highlights a discovery at <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a>, China (the site of the Han capital <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a>) where a hoard of sixteen Roman coins from the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> (14&#8211;37 AD) to <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> (270&#8211;275 AD) have been discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-150">[140]</a></sup> The Roman coins found at &#211;c Eo, Vietnam, near Chinese-controlled <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhou_(region)" title="Jiaozhou (region)">Jiaozhou</a>, date to the mid-2nd century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oreilly_2007_p97_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-oreilly_2007_p97-141">[131]</a></sup> A coin of Maximian (r. 286&#8211;305 AD) was also discovered in <a href="/wiki/Tonkin" title="Tonkin">Tonkin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p153_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p153-143">[133]</a></sup> Roman coins of the 3rd and 4th centuries AD have been discovered in Japan; they were unearthed from <a href="/wiki/Katsuren_Castle" title="Katsuren Castle">Katsuren Castle</a> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uruma,_Okinawa" title="Uruma, Okinawa">Uruma, Okinawa</a>), which was built from the 12th to 15th centuries AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[151]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest gold <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)"><i>solidus</i> coins</a> from the Eastern Roman Empire found in China date to the reign of Byzantine emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> (r. 408&#8211;450 AD) and altogether only forty-eight of them have been found (compared to 1300 silver coins) in <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> and the rest of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p97_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p97-160">[150]</a></sup> The use of silver coins in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a> persisted long after the <a href="/wiki/Tang_campaign_against_Karakhoja" title="Tang campaign against Karakhoja">Tang campaign against Karakhoja</a> and Chinese conquest of 640 AD, with a gradual adoption of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">Chinese bronze coinage</a> during the 7th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p97_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p97-160">[150]</a></sup> Hansen maintains that these Eastern Roman coins were almost always found with <a href="/wiki/Monetary_history_of_Iran" title="Monetary history of Iran">Sasanian Persian silver coins</a> and Eastern Roman gold coins were used more as ceremonial objects like <a href="/wiki/Talisman" title="Talisman">talismans</a>, confirming the pre-eminence of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a> in Chinese Silk Road commerce of Central Asia compared to Eastern Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[152]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scheidel" title="Walter Scheidel">Walter Scheidel</a> remarks that the Chinese viewed Byzantine coins as pieces of exotic jewellery, preferring to use bronze coinage in the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> dynasties, as well as <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">paper money</a> during the Song and Ming periods, even while silver <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a> was plentiful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[153]</a></sup> Ball writes that the scarcity of Roman and Byzantine coins in China, and the greater amounts found in India, suggest that most Chinese silk purchased by the Romans was from maritime India, largely bypassing the overland <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> trade through Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-150">[140]</a></sup> Chinese coins from the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a> and Tang dynasties (6th&#8211;10th centuries AD) have been discovered in India; significantly larger amounts are dated to the Song period (11th&#8211;13th centuries AD), particularly in the territories of the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Chola_dynasty" title="Chola dynasty">Chola dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[154]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even with the Byzantine production of silk starting in the 6th century AD, Chinese varieties were still considered to be of higher quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luttwak_2008_pp168-169_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-luttwak_2008_pp168-169-21">[21]</a></sup> This theory is supported by the discovery of a Byzantine <i>solidus</i> minted during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Justin_II" title="Justin II">Justin II</a> found in a Sui-dynasty tomb of <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> province in 1953, among other Byzantine coins found at various sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luttwak_2008_pp168-169_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-luttwak_2008_pp168-169-21">[21]</a></sup> Chinese histories offer descriptions of Roman and Byzantine coins. The <i><a href="/wiki/Weil%C3%BCe" title="Weil&#252;e">Weil&#252;e</a></i>, <i>Book of the Later Han</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jin" title="Book of Jin">Book of Jin</a></i>, as well as the later <i><a href="/wiki/Wenxian_Tongkao" title="Wenxian Tongkao">Wenxian Tongkao</a></i> noted how ten ancient Roman silver coins were worth one Roman gold coin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-30"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_2004_webpage_37-8"><a href="#cite_note-hill_2004_webpage-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yule_1915_pp43-44_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-yule_1915_pp43-44-72">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[155]</a></sup> The Roman golden <i><a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">aureus</a></i> was worth about twenty-five silver <i><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarii</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[156]</a></sup> During the later Byzantine Empire, twelve silver <i><a href="/wiki/Miliaresion" title="Miliaresion">miliaresion</a></i> was equal to one gold <i><a href="/wiki/Nomisma" title="Nomisma">nomisma</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[157]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Song" title="History of Song">History of Song</a></i> notes that the Byzantines made coins of either silver or gold, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage#early_round_coins" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">without holes in the middle</a>, with an inscription of the king\'s name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-31"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup> It also asserts that the Byzantines forbade the production of counterfeit coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_28-32"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_remains">Human remains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Human remains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> was used to identify that a partial skeleton found in a Roman grave from the 1st or 2nd century AD in Vagnari, Italy, had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian" title="East Asian">East Asian</a> ancestry on his mother\'s side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2016 analysis of archaeological finds from <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a> in London, the site of the ancient Roman city <a href="/wiki/Londinium" title="Londinium">Londinium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, suggests that two or three skeletons from a sample of twenty-two dating to the 2nd to the 4th centuries AD are of Asian ancestry, and possibly of Chinese descent. The assertion is based on forensics and the analysis of skeletal facial features. The discovery has been presented by Dr Rebecca Redfern, curator of human <a href="/wiki/Osteology" title="Osteology">osteology</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_London" title="Museum of London">Museum of London</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[160]</a></sup> No DNA analysis has yet been done, the skull and tooth samples available offer only fragmentary pieces of evidence, and the samples that were used were compared with the morphology of modern populations, not ancient ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[161]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothetical_military_contact">Hypothetical military contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hypothetical military contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sampul_tapestry" title="Sampul tapestry">Sampul tapestry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_European_exploration_of_Asia" title="Chronology of European exploration of Asia">Chronology of European exploration of Asia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margiana-300BCE.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="115" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Margiana-300BCE.png/220px-Margiana-300BCE.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Margiana-300BCE.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margiana-300BCE.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman prisoners of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Battle of Carrhae</a> were brought to <a href="/wiki/Margiana" title="Margiana">Margiana</a> by king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orodes_II_of_Parthia" title="Orodes II of Parthia">Orodes</a>. Their further fate is unknown.</div></div></div>\n<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Homer_H._Dubs" title="Homer H. Dubs">Homer H. Dubs</a> speculated in 1941 that Roman prisoners of war who were transferred to the eastern border of the Parthian empire might later have clashed with Han troops there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[162]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a Roman army under the command of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a> decisively lost the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">battle of Carrhae</a> in 54&#160;BC, an estimated 10,000&#160;Roman prisoners were dispatched by the Parthians to <a href="/wiki/Margiana" title="Margiana">Margiana</a> to man the frontier. Some time later the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> chief <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhizhi" title="Zhizhi">Zhizhi</a> established a state further east in the <a href="/wiki/Talas_River" title="Talas River">Talas valley</a>, near modern-day <a href="/wiki/Taraz" title="Taraz">Taraz</a>. Dubs points to a Chinese account by <a href="/wiki/Ban_Gu" title="Ban Gu">Ban Gu</a> of about "a hundred men" under the command of Zhizhi who fought in a so-called "fish-scale formation" to defend Zhizhi\'s wooden-palisade fortress against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han</a> forces, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zhizhi" title="Battle of Zhizhi">Battle of Zhizhi</a> in 36 BC. He claimed that this might have been the Roman <a href="/wiki/Testudo_formation" title="Testudo formation">testudo formation</a> and that these men, who were captured by the Chinese, founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liqian_village" title="Liqian village">village of Liqian</a> (Li-chien, possibly from "legio") in <a href="/wiki/Yongchang_County" title="Yongchang County">Yongchang County</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[164]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There have been attempts to promote the Sino-Roman connection for tourism, but Dubs\' synthesis of Roman and Chinese sources has not found acceptance among historians, on the grounds that it is highly speculative and reaches too many conclusions without sufficient hard evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_DNA_test" title="Genealogical DNA test">DNA testing</a> in 2005 confirmed the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-European</a> ancestry of a few inhabitants of modern Liqian; this could be explained by transethnic marriages with Indo-European people known to have lived in Gansu in ancient times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_Daily,_20_November_2010_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-China_Daily,_20_November_2010-176">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Telegraph,_23_November_2010_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Telegraph,_23_November_2010-177">[167]</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wusun" title="Wusun">Wusun</a>. A much more comprehensive DNA analysis of more than two hundred male residents of the village in 2007 showed close genetic relation to the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> populace and great deviation from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Eurasian" title="Western Eurasian">Western Eurasian</a> gene pool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Zhou_et_al._2007_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-R._Zhou_et_al._2007-178">[168]</a></sup> The researchers conclude that the people of Liqian are probably of Han Chinese origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Zhou_et_al._2007_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-R._Zhou_et_al._2007-178">[168]</a></sup> The area lacks archaeological evidence of a Roman presence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_Daily,_20_November_2010_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-China_Daily,_20_November_2010-176">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Telegraph,_23_November_2010_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Telegraph,_23_November_2010-177">[167]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="599" decoding="async" height="28" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Terra.png/56px-Terra.png 2x" width="28"/></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:International_relations" title="Portal:International relations">International relations portal</a></span></li>\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="28" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/%E6%98%8E%E5%A4%AA%E5%AE%97.jpg/44px-%E6%98%8E%E5%A4%AA%E5%AE%97.jpg 2x" width="22"/></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:History_of_Imperial_China" title="Portal:History of Imperial China">History of Imperial China portal</a></span></li>\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="548" data-file-width="931" decoding="async" height="19" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SPQRomani.svg/32px-SPQRomani.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SPQRomani.svg/48px-SPQRomani.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SPQRomani.svg/64px-SPQRomani.svg.png 2x" width="32"/></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Ancient_Rome" title="Portal:Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome portal</a></span></li>\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006_01_21_Ath%C3%A8nes_Parth%C3%A9non.JPG"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1565" data-file-width="2115" decoding="async" height="24" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/2006_01_21_Ath%C3%A8nes_Parth%C3%A9non.JPG/32px-2006_01_21_Ath%C3%A8nes_Parth%C3%A9non.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/2006_01_21_Ath%C3%A8nes_Parth%C3%A9non.JPG/48px-2006_01_21_Ath%C3%A8nes_Parth%C3%A9non.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/2006_01_21_Ath%C3%A8nes_Parth%C3%A9non.JPG/64px-2006_01_21_Ath%C3%A8nes_Parth%C3%A9non.JPG 2x" width="32"/></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Classical_civilisation" title="Portal:Classical civilisation">Classical civilisation portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Holy_See_relations" title="China&#8211;Holy See relations">China&#8211;Holy See relations</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Italy_relations" title="China&#8211;Italy relations">China&#8211;Italy relations</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Greece_relations" title="China&#8211;Greece relations">China&#8211;Greece relations</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a>, a contemporaneous ancient kingdom in southern Vietnam</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, 13th-century Venetian merchant and explorer in China</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Malay_Chronicles:_Bloodlines" title="The Malay Chronicles: Bloodlines">Malay Chronicles: Bloodlines</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dragon_Blade_(film)" title="Dragon Blade (film)">Dragon Blade</a></i>, films based on Sino-Roman relations</li>\n<li><i>Empire of Dragons</i>, a novel by <a href="/wiki/Valerio_Massimo_Manfredi" title="Valerio Massimo Manfredi">Valerio Massimo Manfredi</a>, based on a hypothetical contact between the Romans and Chinese.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sino-Roman_relations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For the assertion that the first Chinese mention of Daqin belongs to the <i>Book of the Later Han</i>, see: Wilkinson (2000), p. 730.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hirth (2000) [1885], <a class="external text" href="http://sourcebooks.fordham.edu/halsall/eastasia/romchin1.asp" rel="nofollow">"From the Wei-lio (written before 429 C.E.), for 220&#8211;264 C.E.",</a> (using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wade-Giles" title="Wade-Giles">Wade-Giles</a>) identified these dependent vassal states as <a href="/wiki/Iskandariya" title="Iskandariya">Alexandria-Euphrates</a> or <a href="/wiki/Charax_Spasinu" title="Charax Spasinu">Charax Spasinu</a> ("Ala-san"), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nikephorium&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nikephorium (page does not exist)">Nikephorium</a> ("Lu-fen"), <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a> ("Ch\'ieh-lan"), <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> ("Hsien-tu"), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emesa" title="Emesa">Emesa</a> ("Si-fu"), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hira" title="Hira">Hira</a> ("Ho-lat"). Going south of Palmyra and Emesa led one to the "Stony Land", which Hirth identified as <a href="/wiki/Arabia_Petraea" title="Arabia Petraea">Arabia Petraea</a>, due to the text speaking how it bordered a sea (the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>) where <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a> and real <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearls</a> were extracted. The text also explained the positions of border territories that were controlled by Parthia, such as <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> ("Si-lo"). <br/>Hill (September 2004), <a class="external text" href="http://depts.washington.edu/silkroad/texts/weilue/notes11_30.html" rel="nofollow">"Section 14 &#8211; Roman Dependencies"</a>, identified the dependent vassal states as <a href="/wiki/Azania" title="Azania">Azania</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#28580;&#25955;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zesan</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Tse-san</span></i>), <a href="/wiki/Al_Wajh" title="Al Wajh">Al Wajh</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#39522;&#20998;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">L&#252;fen</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">L&#252;-fen</span></i>), <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Sirhan" title="Wadi Sirhan">Wadi Sirhan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#19988;&#34349;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Qielan</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Ch\'ieh-lan</span></i>), Leukos Lim&#234;n, ancient site controlling the entrance to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aqaba" title="Gulf of Aqaba">Gulf of Aqaba</a> near modern Ayn&#363;nah (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#36066;&#30563;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Xiandu</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Hsien-tu</span></i>), <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#27740;&#24489;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Sifu</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Szu-fu</span></i>), <a href="/wiki/Al-Karak" title="Al-Karak">al-Karak</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#20110;&#32645;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Yuluo</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Y&#252;-lo</span></i>), and <a href="/wiki/Sura_(city)" title="Sura (city)">Sura</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#26031;&#32645;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Siluo</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade&#8211;Giles">Wade&#8211;Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Szu-lo</span></i>).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonian</a>" origin betokens no more than his cultural affinity, and the name Ma&#235;s is Semitic in origin, Cary (1956), p. 130.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The mainstream opinion, noted by Cary (1956), p. 130, note #7, based on the date of <a href="/wiki/Marinus_of_Tyre" title="Marinus of Tyre">Marinus of Tyre</a>, established by his use of many <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajanic</a> foundation names but none identifiable with <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Centuries later <i>Tashkurgan</i> ("Stone Tower") was the capital of the Pamir kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarikol_kingdom" title="Sarikol kingdom">Sarikol</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-yule_1915_p18-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-yule_1915_p18_58-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-yule_1915_p18_58-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Yule (1915), p. 18; for a discussion of <i>Tiaozhi</i> (&#26465;&#25903;) and even its <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> possibly stemming from the <a href="/wiki/Tajiks" title="Tajiks">Tajiks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian peoples</a> under ancient Chinese rule, see footnote #2 on p. 42.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-book_of_later_han_passage-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-book_of_later_han_passage_76-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-book_of_later_han_passage_76-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Fan_Ye_(historian)" title="Fan Ye (historian)">Fan Ye</a>, ed. "&#27704;&#23527;&#20803;&#24180;&#65292;&#25763;&#22283;&#29579;&#38605;&#30001;&#35519;&#24489;&#36963;&#20351;&#32773;&#35427;&#38357;&#26397;&#36032;&#65292;&#29563;&#27138;&#21450;&#24187;&#20154;&#65292;&#33021;&#35722;&#21270;&#21520;&#28779;&#65292;&#33258;&#25903;&#35299;&#65292;&#26131;&#29275;&#39340;&#38957;&#12290;&#21448;&#21892;&#36339;&#20024;&#65292; &#25976;&#20035;&#33267;&#21315;&#12290;&#33258;&#35328;&#25105;&#28023;&#35199;&#20154;&#12290;&#28023;&#35199;&#21363;&#22823;&#31206;&#20063;&#65292;&#25763;&#22283;&#35199;&#21335;&#36890;&#22823;&#31206;&#12290;&#26126;&#24180;&#20803;&#26371;&#65292;&#23433;&#24093;&#20316;&#27138;&#26044;&#24237;&#65292;&#23553;&#38605;&#30001;&#35519;&#29234;&#28450;&#22823;&#37117;&#23561;&#65292;&#36060;&#21360;&#32172;&#65380;&#37329;&#37504;&#65380;&#32181;&#32338;&#21508;&#26377;&#24046;&#20063;&#12290;"<br/>A translation of this passage into English, in addition to an explanation of how Greek athletic performers figured prominently in the neighbouring Parthian and Kushan Empires of Asia, is offered by Christopoulos (August 2012), pp. 40&#8211;41:\n<blockquote><p>The first year of Yongning (120 AD), the southwestern barbarian king of the kingdom of Chan (Burma), Yongyou, proposed illusionists (jugglers) who could metamorphose themselves and spit out fire; they could dismember themselves and change an ox head into a horse head. They were very skilful in acrobatics and they could do a thousand other things. They said that they were from the "west of the seas" (Haixi&#8211;Egypt). The west of the seas is the Daqin (Rome). The Daqin is situated to the south-west of the Chan country. During the following year, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_An_of_Han" title="Emperor An of Han">Andi</a> organized festivities in his country residence and the acrobats were transferred to the Han capital where they gave a performance to the court, and created a great sensation. They received the honours of the Emperor, with gold and silver, and every one of them received a different gift.</p></blockquote></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For information on <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">reestablishment of Western contact with China</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, see: Fontana (2011), pp. 18&#8211;35, 116&#8211;118.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-139"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-139">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a summary of scholarly debate about the possible locations of Cattigara by the end of the 20th century, with proposals ranging from <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mekong_River_Delta" title="Mekong River Delta">Mekong River Delta</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" title="Kingdom of Funan">Kingdom of Funan</a>, see: Su&#225;rez (1999), p. 92.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Cattigara-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Cattigara_140-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mawer also mentions <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kauthara" title="Kauthara">Kauthara</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Kh%C3%A1nh_H%C3%B2a_Province" title="Kh&#225;nh H&#242;a Province">Kh&#225;nh H&#242;a Province</a>, Vietnam) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kutaradja" title="Kutaradja">Kutaradja</a> (<a href="/wiki/Banda_Aceh" title="Banda Aceh">Banda Aceh</a>, Indonesia) as other plausible sites for that port.
